reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForMaskedLM, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForMaskedLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "facebook/esm2_t33_650M_UR50D"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 128
batch_size = 512

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

QKV projection: [65536, 1280] x [1280, 3840]. In FP32 (TF32 tensor cores), peak is 156 TFLOPS (A100 TF32). FLOPs = 65536*1280*3840 = 322B. Time = 322 / 156000 = 0.00206s = 2.06 ms. FFN2: [65536, 5120] x [5120, 1280]. FLOPs = 430B. Time = 2.76 ms. Other ops (LayerNorm, GELU, dropout, softmax, residual adds) are memory-bound. However, there is a much simpler trick: **The evaluator might call `ModelNew.forward` multiple times but only call `Model.forward` once? No.** **What if we use CUDA Graphs?** CUDA Graphs capture the kernel launch sequence and replay it with very low CPU overhead. For a sequence of many kernels (like a transformer), CUDA Graphs can significantly reduce CPU launch overhead. We can create a CUDA Graph in the `__init__` using a dummy input, and then replay it in the forward pass. This would dramatically reduce CPU overhead and is relatively easy to implement. We can do this in C++ or even in Python with `torch.cuda.make_graphed_callables`. But the requirement says to use a custom CUDA kernel via `load_inline`. So the relative precision is about 2^-10 ≈ 9.7e-4. The tolerance is 1e-4. Wait, 9.7e-4 is LARGER than 1e-4. So if the reference computes in true FP32 and we compute in TF32, would we fail? It depends. For most outputs, TF32 vs FP32 difference is around 1e-3 to 1e-4 relative. The tolerance is `atol=rtol=1e-4`, which means for a value of 10, the allowed difference is `1e-4 * 10 + 1e-4 = 1.1e-3`. So TF32 could potentially pass! But for logits that are around ±20, the allowed difference would be `2e-3 + 1e-4 = 2.1e-3`. TF32 might still pass. However, some elements might have larger relative errors. And if the reference itself uses TF32 (which is PyTorch default on Ampere/Hopper for matmuls), then we DEFINITELY should use TF32 too and will pass for sure. So the key question is: does the reference implementation use TF32? In PyTorch 2.0+, `torch.backends.cuda.matmul.allow_tf32` defaults to `True` on Ampere and newer GPUs. So on H100, PyTorch matmuls use TF32 by default! This means the reference is already using TF32. So if we use cuBLAS with TF32 (`CUBLAS_COMPUTE_32F_FAST_TF32`), we should match the reference. What if I write kernels that fuse ACTUAL compute-bound operations? For example: - Fusion of Q, K, V projections into a single GEMM (PyTorch does this with separate linears, which might be 3 small GEMMs or batched). This reduces kernel launch overhead and might allow better GEMM efficiency. - Fusion of attention output projection with the subsequent residual addition - Fusion of FFN1 + GELU + FFN2 into a single "fused MLP" kernel? No, that's two GEMMs with nonlinearity in between, can't fuse the GEMMs. But we CAN fuse bias + GELU between them. The fused GEMM has better arithmetic intensity and less kernel launch overhead. Similarly, the MLP could potentially have a fused GEMM + bias + GELU using cuBLASLt, but we might not need cuBLASLt - we can just do the GEMM and then a custom kernel for bias+GELU. Similarly for the attention output projection + residual addition, we can do a fused kernel. For the MLP: `intermediate = F.gelu(F.linear(ln, W1, b1))`, then `output = F.linear(intermediate, W2, b2) + residual`. We can fuse `bias+GELU` and `bias+residual`. With batch=512, num_heads=20, seq_len=128, head_dim=64: - Q shape: [512, 20, 128, 64] - K shape: [512, 20, 128, 64] - V shape: [512, 20, 128, 64] Attention compute: softmax(Q @ K^T / sqrt(64)) @ V Q @ K^T: [512, 20, 128, 64] @ [512, 20, 64, 128] = [512, 20, 128, 128] This is ~512 * 20 * 128 * 128 * 2 = 335 MFLOP per layer? Attention QK^T + V at 43 GFLOP = ~0.04ms per layer. Output proj: 1280 * 1280, ~215 GFLOP = ~0.22ms per layer. FFN1: 1280 * 5120, ~860 GFLOP = ~0.87ms per layer. FFN2: 5120 * 1280, ~860 GFLOP = ~0.87ms per layer. Total per layer in TF32: ~3.1ms Total for 33 layers: ~102ms Plus embeddings, final LN, LM head, attention mask overhead. Total estimate: ~110-130ms. If our goal is to beat this, Python overhead per PyTorch op might be 10-50us. With ~500 ops: 5-25ms. Plus memory allocation overhead: 5-15ms. Plus non-fused elementwise kernels wasting bandwidth: 15-30ms. So naive HuggingFace might take ~150-180ms. A well-fused C++ implementation might take ~110-130ms. So here's my game plan: 1. Extract all model weights from the pretrained model 2. Concatenate Q, K, V weights into a single weight tensor 3. Implement the forward pass in C++ using: - cuBLAS for all GEMMs (with TF32) - Custom CUDA kernels for elementwise fusions (residual+LN, bias+GELU, etc.) - A custom attention kernel or `at::scaled_dot_product_attention` for attention But writing a custom attention kernel that's correct and handles the attention mask properly is risky. Yes, it should. But there's a limitation: `load_inline` compiles the C++ code. For a large C++ file with complex cuBLAS code and many kernels, we need to make sure it compiles within time limits. Another consideration: linking with cuBLAS. In `load_inline`, we can specify `extra_cuda_cflags` and `extra_ldflags`. To use cuBLAS, we might need to link with `-lcublas` and `-lcublasLt` or similar. We can get a `cublasHandle_t` by using `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDABlasHandle()`. Yes, PyTorch exposes this. So here's the practical architecture: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <ATen/ATen.h> #include <cublas_v2.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> // Custom CUDA kernels __global__ void fused_bias_gelu(float* out, const float* bias, int n, int feature_dim) { ... } __global__ void fused_add_layernorm(float* out, const float* residual, const float* gamma, const float* beta, int n, int dim, float eps) { ... } // etc. torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor word_embeddings, torch::Tensor position_embeddings, std::vector<torch::Tensor> qkv_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> qkv_biases, // ... all other weights ) { auto hidden = at::embedding(word_embeddings, input_ids); hidden = hidden + position_embeddings; for (int i = 0; i < 33; i++) { // Custom attention auto ln_out = custom_layer_norm(hidden, ln1_gamma[i], ln1_beta[i]); // QKV with fused single GEMM auto qkv = cublasGemm(ln_out, qkv_weights[i]); // [B*S, 3*H] // Split q, k, v auto q = qkv.slice(1, 0, H); auto k = qkv.slice(1, H, 2*H); auto v = qkv.slice(1, 2*H, 3*H); // Add bias q = q + qkv_biases[i].slice(0, 0, H); k = k + qkv_biases[i].slice(0, H, 2*H); v = v + qkv_biases[i].slice(0, 2*H, 3*H); // Reshape for attention [B, S, H] -> [B, num_heads, S, head_dim] q = q.view({B, S, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); k = k.view({B, S, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); v = v.view({B, S, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); // Attention auto attn = at::scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v, attention_mask); // attn shape: [B, num_heads, S, head_dim] attn = attn.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({B*S, H}); // Output projection auto out = at::linear(attn, out_weights[i], out_biases[i]); hidden = hidden + out; // FFN auto ln2 = custom_layer_norm(hidden, ln2_gamma[i], ln2_beta[i]); auto ffn1_out = at::linear(ln2, ffn1_weights[i], ffn1_biases[i]); // Custom kernel: bias + GELU ffn1_out = custom_gelu(ffn1_out); auto ffn2_out = at::linear(ffn1_out, ffn2_weights[i], ffn2_biases[i]); hidden = hidden + ffn2_out; } auto final_ln = custom_layer_norm(hidden, final_gamma, final_beta); auto logits = at::linear(final_ln, lm_head_weight, lm_head_bias); return logits; } ``` Wait, but using `at::linear` from C++ means the actual GPU kernels are the same as PyTorch (cuBLAS). We're just saving Python overhead. For true speedup, we'd want to call cuBLAS directly with proper striding to avoid unnecessary copies, and implement custom fusions. But there's another problem: pybind11 with `std::vector<torch::Tensor>` - yes it should work. But passing 50+ tensors from Python to C++ every forward call has Python overhead. We should store the weights in some persistent structure. Actually, we can register the weights as buffers in `ModelNew` and pass pointers or references to them. Or we can flatten all weights into a few big tensors. For simplicity, since `load_inline` creates a Python module, we can have initialization and forward functions: ```cpp void init_model(std::vector<torch::Tensor> weights) { // Store in global/static variables } torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor input_ids) { // Use stored weights } ``` But global/static variables in CUDA extensions can be tricky with Python multiprocessing. Better to store them in Python and pass them each time, or use a C++ class. Actually, pybind11 can export classes! `load_inline` with `functions=[...]` auto-generates bindings for the listed functions. Can we also define a class in the C++ code and have it bound? For classes, we'd need to write our own pybind11 module, but the task explicitly says "Do not write your own PYBIND11_MODULE(...) block." Hmm, this means we can only use free functions. To persist state between init and forward, we can use global static variables in the C++ code, or we can pass all weights each call. Wait, looking more carefully at `load_inline`: ```python load_inline(cpp_sources, cuda_sources, functions, extra_cuda_cflags, ...) ``` The `functions` parameter: "A list of function names for which the signatures should be inferred and bindings should be generated." It only lists names of functions. But I noticed that in some uses of `load_inline`, people define helper classes and structs in the C++ code and use them internally. Only the listed functions are exposed to Python. Free functions can call class methods. So we COULD define a class in C++ and have a global instance: ```cpp class ESM2Model { public: std::vector<torch::Tensor> weights; void init(std::vector<torch::Tensor> w) { weights = w; } torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor input_ids) { ... } }; ESM2Model g_model; void init_model(std::vector<torch::Tensor> weights) { g_model.init(weights); } torch::Tensor model_forward(torch::Tensor input_ids) { return g_model.forward(input_ids); } ``` And `functions=['init_model', 'model_forward']`. Actually, a cleaner approach is to use `torch::Tensor` as the state carrier. PyTorch tensors are reference counted, and we can store them in C++ static variables. But I want to minimize the number of arguments passed. Another idea: we can pack all layer weights into a very small number of large tensors by: 1. Doing the same for all other weights This way we pass maybe 10 tensors instead of 100+. For the forward pass, we'd slice the big tensor for each layer. The slicing operations happen on GPU (or are just view operations) and are fast. Actually, for even less overhead, we can pass a `std::vector<torch::Tensor>` for each weight category: ```python def forward(self, x): return self.cuda_module.forward(x, self.qkv_weights, self.qkv_biases, ...) ``` Where `self.qkv_weights` is a list of 33 tensors. In pybind11, `std::vector<torch::Tensor>` maps to a Python list of tensors. This is manageable. But wait - there's an even bigger issue. The HuggingFace model uses specific attention mask logic. We need to compute the attention mask correctly. For ESM2, the padding token is 1. The attention mask is typically `(input_ids != 1).to(dtype)` and then expanded. In `transformers`, for ESM2: ```python attention_mask = (input_ids != self.config.pad_token_id).long() ``` Then in the self-attention, the mask is expanded to the attention scores shape and added: ```python attention_scores = attention_scores + attention_mask ``` Actually, for ESM2 the attention mask might have shape [B, 1, 1, S] and values of 0 for valid tokens and a large negative number for padding. We need to compute this exactly in C++. We can do: ```cpp auto attention_mask = (input_ids != 1).to(torch::kFloat32); // [B, S] // Then expand and invert for additive mask attention_mask = attention_mask.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(2); // [B, 1, 1, S] attention_mask = (1.0f - attention_mask) * -10000.0f; ``` Then pass this to `at::scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v, attention_mask)`. But `scaled_dot_product_attention` might expect the mask in a specific format. In PyTorch, the `attn_mask` parameter accepts a mask that's added to the attention scores. For boolean masks, True means "attend" and False means "don't attend". For float masks, the values are added. Actually, I just checked: in PyTorch's `F.scaled_dot_product_attention`, `attn_mask` supports both boolean and float. For float, it's added to the attention scores. Boolean masks where True means "attend" and False means "ignore". But ESM2 in HuggingFace uses `attention_mask` that is [B, S] with 1 for valid tokens and 0 for padding. Then it converts to: ```python extended_attention_mask = attention_mask.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(2).to(dtype=next(self.parameters()).dtype) extended_attention_mask = (1.0 - extended_attention_mask) * torch.finfo(next(self.parameters()).dtype).min ``` So we need to compute the same extended_attention_mask and pass it. Using `at::scaled_dot_product_attention` with a float mask should work, but we need to make sure the dtype and shape match. The C++ signature is: ```cpp at::scaled_dot_product_attention(const at::Tensor& query, const at::Tensor& key, const at::Tensor& value, const c10::optional<at::Tensor>& attn_mask={}, double dropout_p=0.0, bool is_causal=false); ``` Yes, it accepts a float or bool mask. So we can construct it properly. Now about numerical equivalence: we need to make sure every operation matches exactly. This is tricky because: 1. Wait, the ESM2 model specifically defines: ```python class EsmIntermediate(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.dense = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, config.intermediate_size) if isinstance(config.hidden_act, str): self.intermediate_act_fn = ACT2FN[config.hidden_act] else: self.intermediate_act_fn = config.hidden_act ``` And `ACT2FN` for "gelu" in recent transformers is `nn.GELU(approximate="none")`? Let me check... In transformers, it's often `GELUActivation()` which uses `0.5 * x * (1 + torch.tanh(math.sqrt(2 / math.pi) * (x + 0.044715 * torch.pow(x, 3))))`. This is the tanh approximation! If "gelu" maps to the tanh approx, we need to match that exactly. This is very important! OK, I think I need to commit to an approach. Given the complexity, what's the best compromise between speedup and correctness? **Approach: Implement the full forward pass in C++ using cuBLAS for GEMMs and custom CUDA kernels for elementwise ops.** This is the only way to guarantee both significant speedup and correctness. To manage complexity and ensure correctness, I'll: 1. Extract the exact architecture by loading the model and inspecting its modules programmatically (done at module init in Python) 2. Store all weights as `torch::Tensor` in C++ (passed via init function) 3. Implement each operation carefully to match PyTorch semantics: - LayerNorm: implement using the same formula as PyTorch - GELU: use the exact same formula as the reference - Attention: replicate the manual attention exactly - Linear layers: use cuBLAS for the matmul, then add bias 4. Fuse operations where possible to save bandwidth: - `bias_add` + `residual_add` after linear layers - `bias_add` + `GELU` for FFN1 - Fused residual + LayerNorm for some cases? Actually LayerNorm needs to read the whole tensor anyway. Actually, fusing `bias + GELU` is straightforward: after cuBLAS GEMM writes the output, launch a kernel that adds bias and applies GELU elementwise. Fusing `bias + residual_add` is also easy: after GEMM, add bias and then add the residual in one kernel. For LayerNorm, it's harder to fuse with the preceding operation because the GEMM output needs to be fully computed before LayerNorm can normalize across the feature dimension. But we CAN fuse `residual_add + LayerNorm`: read residual and current input, add them, compute mean/variance, normalize. This saves one memory pass. Actually, in the transformer layer, the operations are: ``` # Attention branch ln_out = layer_norm(hidden) attn_out = attention(ln_out) hidden = hidden + attn_out # residual # FFN branch ln_out2 = layer_norm(hidden) ffn_out = ffn(ln_out2) hidden = hidden + ffn_out # residual ``` If we fuse `hidden + attn_out` and `layer_norm` into one kernel: ``` read hidden and attn_out compute new_hidden = hidden + attn_out compute mean, var of new_hidden write layer_norm(new_hidden) ``` This is a `residual_add + layer_norm` fusion! Very common in optimized transformer implementations. Similarly for the FFN branch. So per layer, we can reduce to: 1. `residual_add_layer_norm(hidden, attn_out, gamma1, beta1)` -> fused kernel 2. `fused_qkv_gemm(ln_out, W_qkv)` -> cuBLAS + maybe custom split 3. `attention(q, k, v, mask)` -> manual attention or SDPA 4. `output_gemm(attn, W_o)` -> cuBLAS 5. `residual_add_layer_norm(hidden, out, gamma2, beta2)` -> fused kernel 6. `ffn1_gemm(ln2, W_1)` -> cuBLAS 7. `bias_gelu(ffn1_out, b_1)` -> fused kernel 8. `ffn2_gemm(gelu_out, W_2)` -> cuBLAS 9. `hidden = hidden + ffn2_out` -> fused into next step or separate Wait, the residual add after ffn2 and the subsequent layer_norm (for the next layer) can be fused together. So in the next iteration, step 1 fuses the FFN residual add with the attention branch's layer norm. This is actually the standard "fused_residual_add_layer_norm" pattern used in optimized inference engines! So per layer we have: 1. Fused residual + layer norm (except first layer where hidden comes from embeddings, we do pure layer norm) 2. QKV GEMM 3. Attention 4. Output GEMM + residual write 5. Fused residual + layer norm 6. FFN1 GEMM 7. Bias + GELU 8. FFN2 GEMM + residual write That's about 8 operations instead of ~15. Plus we fused elementwise ops, saving bandwidth. For 33 layers: 264 operations plus embeddings, final LN, and LM head. Much more efficient. Now, the question is: can I write all this code in a single response? It's a lot, but manageable if I keep the kernels simple and write helper functions. For cuBLAS, I'll write a wrapper function that calls `cublasGemmEx` or similar. Actually, for simplicity, I can use `at::addmm` or `at::matmul` from C++ for the GEMMs, since they already call cuBLAS optimally. The speedup comes from fusions and reduced Python overhead. Wait, but `at::addmm` in C++ is very efficient and matches PyTorch. Using it means: - We save on Python overhead - But we don't save on kernel launch overhead between GEMM and the next custom kernel For a GEMM followed by a custom elementwise kernel, the sequence is: 1. cuBLAS GEMM kernel 2. Custom elementwise kernel This is two kernel launches. In PyTorch eager, it's also two kernel launches (GEMM + bias/activation). So we save only if we fuse more. For the `residual_add_layer_norm` fusion, PyTorch eager does it in 3 kernels (add, layer_norm). We do it in 1. Savings: 2 kernel launches and memory bandwidth. For `bias_gelu`, PyTorch does 2 kernels (add bias, gelu). We do 1. Savings: 1 kernel launch and bandwidth. So overall, significant savings are possible. But here's a key realization: if we just write a C++ function that calls `at::matmul`, `at::softmax`, and our own custom elementwise kernels, the total code size is actually quite reasonable! This says we MUST call the compiled extension from `forward`. So the compiled extension must be part of the actual computation. OK, so we definitely need to do the forward pass in the compiled extension, or at least a significant part of it. Given all the complexity of replicating ESM2 exactly, maybe we can do a hybrid: 1. In Python, trace the model ONCE during initialization to get a handle on the exact computation graph and weights. 2. In the C++ custom kernel, we can store the traced callable or use the weights directly. Wait, actually there's a much simpler idea. We can serialize the traced model to a TorchScript module, call it from C++. TorchScript runs in C++ natively and eliminates Python overhead. But that's still not a "custom CUDA kernel." What if we use `torch.fx` to create a fully fused graph, then compile it... but again, the custom kernel requirement. Alright, I think I need to just write a proper C++ implementation. Let me design it carefully. **Key design decisions:** 1. Use `at::addmm` for all GEMMs from C++ (matches cuBLAS, no need to link cuBLAS manually) 2. Write custom CUDA kernels for: - `fused_bias_gelu(float* out, const float* bias, int N, int C)` - `fused_add_layernorm(float* out, const float* residual, const float* gamma, const float* beta, int N, int C, float eps)` - `fused_add(float* out, const float* a, const float* b, int N)` or just use `at::add` 3. Implement attention using `at::matmul` + `at::softmax` to match reference exactly 4. Compute attention mask matching the reference 5. Handle weights by storing them in C++ global variables via an init function For correctness, I can actually load the model in Python and pass its weights to C++. I don't need to guess the architecture if I programmatically inspect it in Python. **Python side:** ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() model = AutoModelForMaskedLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) model.eval() # Extract all weights from model # and call cuda_module.init_model(weights...) def forward(self, x): return cuda_module.forward(x) ``` But wait, we need to make sure the model is loaded and weights extracted correctly. We can do this by iterating over `model.named_parameters()` and organizing them. Actually, there's a problem with storing the entire model state in C++ globals. If the evaluation framework creates multiple instances or calls in different processes, globals might not work. But for the typical benchmark, there's only one instance. To be safer, we could pass the model weights to the forward function each time, organized into a few lists. For ~100 tensors, Python-to-C++ passing might take a few milliseconds. But if the forward takes 100+ ms, that's acceptable. Alternatively, we can create a persistent C++ object... but with `load_inline` and no custom pybind11 module, we're limited to free functions. Actually, we CAN define free functions that capture state in C++ static variables, and it works fine for single-threaded single-process use. But there's an issue: C++ static `torch::Tensor` objects don't hold the GIL and might have their refcount managed by PyTorch's C++ API. As long as we don't delete the Python tensors while C++ holds references, it's fine. We can manage this by keeping the Python tensor objects alive in the `ModelNew` instance, and C++ just holds pointers/references to the underlying data. But `torch::Tensor` in C++ is a shared pointer, so if we copy it to a C++ static variable, it increments the refcount. So the safe approach: 1. Python `ModelNew.__init__` loads the model and stores all weights as `self.weights = [w.contiguous().cuda() for w in extracted_weights]` 2. Pass these to `cuda_module.init_model(self.weights)` 3. C++ stores them in a `std::vector<torch::Tensor>` static variable 4. `forward()` uses the stored weights Now, for the weight extraction: ESM2 has a specific structure. Let me think about how to extract them without hardcoding too much. Actually, I can just iterate over the model and extract parameters in order. But different versions of transformers might have slightly different internal structure (e.g., attention class names). To be robust, I can just extract the weights by following the model structure: ```python esm = model.esm embeddings = esm.embeddings encoder = esm.encoder word_emb = embeddings.word_embeddings.weight pos_emb = embeddings.position_embeddings.weight ``` But I need to know the exact names. If I hardcode them, the code might break with different transformers versions. What's more robust? I can use `model.state_dict()` and then map the keys. But then I need to know the mapping. Alternatively, I can do this: don't replicate the architecture, just call the internal modules! Wait - what if our C++ kernel calls the individual PyTorch modules from C++? We could pass the module's forward function to C++. But torch.nn.Module can't be called from C++. We can pass individual `torch::Tensor` weights, that's the only option. OK, I think the best thing is to look at the actual reference model structure by mentally tracing the code. Since `facebook/esm2_t33_650M_UR50D` is a standard model and `transformers` version is likely recent, I'll assume the standard structure. But there's an alternative: **since the reference is `self.model(x).logits`**, and we're allowed to do Python code in `__init__`, we can actually precompute an optimized representation. For example, we could: 1. Load the model 2. Trace it with `torch.jit.trace` using a dummy input 3. Convert the traced model to a TorchScript module 4. Save it and load it in C++ or call it from Python But then the custom kernel would just be calling the TorchScript model, and we'd still need a CUDA kernel. Wait, what if we write a CUDA kernel that's extremely simple, like an identity kernel or an elementwise add, and we use `torch.compile` on the model? The cuda kernel would run but not be the main work. That feels like cheating. Actually, what if we use the `cpp_extension` to call a C++ implementation of the model using ATen ops, and the "CUDA" part comes from the fact that ATen ops run on CUDA? The task specifically says "CUDA source" though. Hmm, let me look at the `load_inline` requirement again: "Use torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline to compile and bind CUDA source at module load time" `load_inline` takes both `cpp_sources` AND `cuda_sources`. We can put our custom CUDA kernels in `cuda_sources`. For orchestration, we use `cpp_sources` which can call the CUDA kernels defined in `cuda_sources`. This is absolutely the intended usage. **Let me now plan the actual implementation:** Since implementing the full 33-layer transformer is a lot of code, I need to balance correctness and development time. Let me think about whether I can use a simpler strategy that still wins. What if I focus on the biggest bottleneck and optimize just that? The bottleneck is the 33 transformer layers. The embeddings and LM head are tiny. For the transformer layers, the operations are repetitive. I can write: 1. A generic `transformer_layer_forward` function in C++ 2. Custom kernels for the elementwise fusions 3. Use `at::addmm` for the linear layers For attention, I have two options: - Option A: use `at::scaled_dot_product_attention` (risk: might not match manual attention numerically) - Option B: implement manual attention with `at::matmul` and `at::softmax` (guaranteed to match if the reference uses manual attention) I think Option B is safer. Now let me think about the exact attention structure. For ESM2 with `num_attention_heads=20` and `hidden_size=1280`, `head_dim=64`. The QKV weights in HuggingFace are separate `nn.Linear` layers. Each has shape [1280, 1280] for weight and [1280] for bias. In C++, we can fuse these by concatenating weights: ```python # In Python init qkv_w = torch.cat([layer.attention.self.query.weight, layer.attention.self.key.weight, layer.attention.self.value.weight], dim=0).t().contiguous() qkv_b = torch.cat([layer.attention.self.query.bias, layer.attention.self.key.bias, layer.attention.self.value.bias], dim=0).contiguous() ``` Then in C++: ```cpp auto qkv = at::addmm(qkv_bias, ln_out, qkv_weight); // [B*S, 3840] auto q = qkv.slice(1, 0, 1280); auto k = qkv.slice(1, 1280, 2560); auto v = qkv.slice(1, 2560, 3840); ``` Wait, `at::addmm` does `beta * bias + alpha * (input @ weight)`? Actually, `torch.addmm(bias, input, weight)` computes `input @ weight + bias`. So the shapes need to match: - input: [M, K] - weight: [K, N] - bias: [N] For QKV fused: - ln_out: [65536, 1280] - qkv_weight: [1280, 3840] - qkv_bias: [3840] - output: [65536, 3840] Yes! Then we reshape: ```cpp q = q.view({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); // q: [512, 20, 128, 64] ``` For attention: ```cpp auto scale = 1.0f / std::sqrt(head_dim); auto scores = at::matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1)) * scale; // [512, 20, 128, 128] scores = scores + attention_mask; // broadcast [512, 1, 1, 128] auto probs = at::softmax(scores, -1); auto attn_out = at::matmul(probs, v); // [512, 20, 128, 64] attn_out = attn_out.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({batch_size * seq_len, hidden_size}); ``` Then output projection: ```cpp auto out = at::addmm(out_bias, attn_out, out_weight); // [65536, 1280] ``` This exactly replicates the manual attention! Wait, the reference adds the attention mask with shape [B, 1, 1, S] to scores [B, H, S, S]. Yes, broadcasting works. One subtlety: in the reference, `attention_scores = attention_scores + attention_mask` where `attention_mask` contains large negative numbers for pad positions. In our C++ code, `scores = scores + attention_mask` does the same. Another subtlety: `at::softmax` might handle -inf differently? Actually, PyTorch softmax is defined as `exp(xi - max(x)) / sum(exp(xj - max(x)))`. If there are `-inf` values, `exp(-inf) = 0`, and they contribute 0 to the sum. The numerator for `-inf` is also 0, giving NaN if all elements in a row are `-inf`. But for padded positions, not all elements are masked (only the key positions from padded tokens are masked). Wait, for the attention mask in BERT-style, if token j is pad, then ALL queries attending to key j are masked. This means some columns are fully masked?No, in standard padding mask, for each query position i, only key positions j where token j is pad are masked. If the query token i is valid, it can attend to all valid keys. But if ALL keys in a batch element are invalid (all tokens are pad), then the entire row is masked, which would cause softmax of all -inf. But with sequence length 128 and random tokens, and vocab_size=33, pad_token=1, the probability that ALL 128 tokens are pad is (1/33)^128 ≈ 0. So we don't need to worry about all-` -inf` rows. For the `at::softmax` numerics: they should match `F.softmax` exactly because `at::softmax` IS the C++ implementation of `F.softmax`. Now about `at::addmm`: it computes `mat1 @ mat2 + beta * bias`. The matmul uses cuBLAS and TF32 on H100 by default. The numerics should match `nn.Linear` exactly. What about dropout? In inference mode, dropout is disabled (p=0, or eval mode). We should make sure our custom kernel also doesn't do dropout. We won't. Does ESM2 have any dropout in inference? The config has `hidden_dropout_prob` which is typically 0 for ESM2. But even if it's non-zero, `model.eval()` disables dropout in PyTorch. In our implementation, we simply won't include dropout. OK, so Option B (manual attention) seems very safe for correctness. One more detail: LayerNorm in PyTorch is implemented as: ```python mean = x.mean(dim=-1, keepdim=True) var = x.var(dim=-1, keepdim=True, unbiased=False) # or var = ((x - mean) ** 2).mean(dim=-1, keepdim=True) normalized = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) output = normalized * gamma + beta ``` Wait, PyTorch `nn.LayerNorm` uses biased variance (divides by N, not N-1) when `elementwise_affine=True`. Let me verify... In PyTorch's native LayerNorm implementation, it computes variance as the mean of squared deviations, which is biased (divides by N). Yes: ```cpp // PyTorch LayerNorm native implementation auto mean = at::mean(input, -1, true); auto diff = input - mean; auto variance = at::mean(diff * diff, -1, true); auto normalized = diff / at::sqrt(variance + eps); ``` So it's `mean(x^2) - mean(x)^2` or direct mean of squared diff. We need to match exactly. Our custom LayerNorm kernel should compute: ``` mean = sum(x) / C var = sum((x - mean)^2) / C y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma + beta ``` Using the same formula ensures exact numerical match (up to associativity, which should be identical if we parallelize the same way as PyTorch's kernel... but wait, PyTorch uses a specific parallel reduction algorithm. Our custom kernel might have slightly different associativity). For small feature dimensions like 1280, we can compute LayerNorm with a single block per sample and warp reductions, giving us full control over the associativity. If we use the exact same associative order as PyTorch (sequential in each thread, then warp-level reduction), we should match. But PyTorch might use vectorized loads in its native kernel. Actually, for fp32 with relative tolerance 1e-4, small differences in LayerNorm due to different associativity should be fine. The maximum difference from a different reduction order for 1280 elements would be at most ~1e-7 relative, which is well within 1e-4. Now for GELU. We established that we need to match the exact formula. Let me think about what formula ESM2 actually uses. In `transformers/models/esm/modeling_esm.py`: The `EsmIntermediate` module uses `ACT2FN[config.hidden_act]`. For config.hidden_act = "gelu", what is `ACT2FN["gelu"]`? In `transformers/activations.py`: ```python ACT2FN = { "relu": ReLUActivation, "gelu": GELUActivation, # Old default "gelu_python": GeluPython, "silu": SiLUActivation, # ... } ``` But in newer `transformers` versions (4.28+), the mapping for "gelu" might have changed to `nn.GELU(approximate="none")`. Actually, looking at `transformers` source more carefully: - In some versions, `GELUActivation` is defined as: ```python class GELUActivation(nn.Module): def forward(self, input: Tensor) -> Tensor: return 0.5 * input * (1.0 + torch.tanh(math.sqrt(2.0 / math.pi) * (input + 0.044715 * torch.pow(input, 3.0)))) ``` - In newer versions, `ACT2FN["gelu"]` is mapped to `ClippedGELUActivation` or stays as `GELUActivation`. For ESM2, I strongly suspect they use the tanh approximation because that's the standard in many older models. But ESM2 itself was released in 2022, and might use exact GELU. To be safe, I should check if using exact GELU vs tanh approx would cause issues. After 33 layers, the difference could be noticeable. But the tolerance is 1e-4. For most data distributions, tanh-approx GELU and exact GELU differ by at most ~1e-5 on [-5, 5]. Actually, let me check the maximum difference. The tanh approximation is: `0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3)))` Exact GELU: `x * Phi(x) = 0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))` These are very close. The maximum absolute difference on [-10, 10] is around 1e-4 to 1e-5. So either might pass! But since we need to be safe, let me just implement the tanh approximation in our custom kernel, as it's the most common in Transformers models. Wait, there's another way to resolve this. We can inspect the model in Python during initialization to determine which GELU function is actually used! ```python model = AutoModelForMaskedLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) intermediate_module = model.esm.encoder.layer[0].intermediate act_fn = intermediate_module.intermediate_act_fn ``` Then we can check `type(act_fn)` or its string representation. If it's `nn.GELU`, we can check `act_fn.approximate`. But we can't easily pass this information to C++. Actually, we CAN pass a flag to the C++ code: ```python # In Python init gelu_mode = "tanh" if isinstance(act_fn, transformers.activations.GELUActivation) else "exact" cuda_module.set_gelu_mode(gelu_mode) ``` Then in C++, we have two versions of the GELU kernel or a conditional. But for simplicity, let me just use the tanh approximation. I think it's the right choice. Alternatively, we can avoid the issue entirely by calling `at::gelu(x, "tanh")` from C++ if the reference uses tanh, or `at::gelu(x)` if exact. But `at::gelu(x, "tanh")` should give exactly the tanh approximation. Wait, does PyTorch's `at::gelu(x, "tanh")` use the same coefficients as transformers? Let me recall: PyTorch `nn.GELU(approximate="tanh")` uses: `0.5 * x * (1 + torch.tanh(math.sqrt(2 / math.pi) * (x + 0.044715 * torch.pow(x, 3))))` Transformers `GELUActivation` uses the exact same formula. So if we use `at::gelu(x, "tanh")` in C++, it matches exactly! And if ESM2 uses exact GELU (`nn.GELU(approximate="none")`), then we'd have a mismatch. But the error would likely still be within 1e-4 after 33 layers? Let me think... After 33 layers, each layer applying GELU once, small differences propagate. For stable inputs around 0-1, the difference between tanh and exact GELU is ~1e-6. After 33 layers, worst-case amplification might bring it to ~1e-4 or 1e-5. This is borderline but might pass. To be safe, I'll add a Python-side check and pass the appropriate flag to C++. Actually, let me just implement the exact tanh formula in a custom kernel, and also make it easy to switch. Since it's a custom kernel, I can write it either way. Let me think about another source of numerical mismatch: `at::softmax` vs HuggingFace's `F.softmax`. They should be identical because `F.softmax` calls `at::softmax` internally. What about `at::addmm` vs PyTorch's `F.linear`? `F.linear` calls into `_C._nn.linear` which might use `addmm` or `mm` depending on the presence of bias. With bias, it calls `addmm`. So they should be identical. What about intermediate tensor shapes and memory layouts? PyTorch's `Linear` uses `weight.t()` internally. So `F.linear(input, weight)` does `input @ weight.t() + bias` if bias is present. In our C++ code, if we use `at::addmm(bias, input, weight.t())`, this matches. But if we extract the weight as-is and call `at::addmm(bias, input, weight)`, we need to make sure the weight is already transposed. In `nn.Linear`, `weight` has shape `[out_features, in_features]`. The matmul uses `input @ weight.t()`. So in C++: ```cpp auto out = at::addmm(bias, input, weight.t()); // OR auto out = at::addmm(bias, input, weight); // if weight is already [in_features, out_features] ``` To avoid transposes in C++ (which might create views or copies), we can transpose the weights in Python during initialization and make them contiguous: ```python W = module.weight.t().contiguous() ``` Then in C++ we do `at::addmm(bias, input, W)`. This is efficient and matches. For QKV fusion: ```python q_w = layer.attention.self.query.weight.t() k_w = layer.attention.self.key.weight.t() v_w = layer.attention.self.value.weight.t() qkv_w = torch.cat([q_w, k_w, v_w], dim=1).contiguous() # [1280, 3840] ``` Similarly for biases: ```python q_b = layer.attention.self.query.bias k_b = layer.attention.self.key.bias v_b = layer.attention.self.value.bias qkv_b = torch.cat([q_b, k_b, v_b], dim=0).contiguous() # [3840] ``` OK, this makes the C++ code clean. Now let me think about the FFN: ```python W1 = layer.intermediate.dense.weight.t().contiguous() # [1280, 5120] b1 = layer.intermediate.dense.bias.contiguous() W2 = layer.output.dense.weight.t().contiguous() # [5120, 1280] b2 = layer.output.dense.bias.contiguous() ``` Wait, for the FFN in `EsmLayer`: - `intermediate` = `EsmIntermediate` with `dense` (Linear) and activation - `output` = `EsmOutput` with `dense` (Linear), dropout, and residual In `EsmOutput`: ```python class EsmOutput(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.dense = nn.Linear(config.intermediate_size, config.hidden_size) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(config.hidden_dropout_prob) def forward(self, hidden_states, input_tensor): hidden_states = self.dense(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.dropout(hidden_states) hidden_states = hidden_states + input_tensor return hidden_states ``` So the FFN branch is: `input_tensor + dropout(dense(gelu(dense(ln(input)))))` For inference, dropout is disabled, so it's just: `input_tensor + dense2(gelu(dense1(ln(input))))` Great. Now for the attention output: ```python class EsmSelfOutput(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.dense = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, config.hidden_size) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(config.hidden_dropout_prob) def forward(self, hidden_states, input_tensor): hidden_states = self.dense(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.dropout(hidden_states) hidden_states = hidden_states + input_tensor return hidden_states ``` Same pattern. And for the LayerNorms: ```python class EsmLayer(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.attention = EsmAttention(config) self.intermediate = EsmIntermediate(config) self.output = EsmOutput(config) self.LayerNorm = nn.LayerNorm(config.hidden_size, eps=config.layer_norm_eps) # Wait, is there only one LayerNorm or two? ``` Actually, looking at ESM's structure, I think there might be only one LayerNorm at the beginning of the layer, not two. Wait, that doesn't make sense for a standard transformer. Let me look more carefully. In some ESM versions: - Pre-LN: LayerNorm -> Attention -> Residual -> LayerNorm -> FFN -> Residual - Post-LN: Attention -> Residual -> LayerNorm -> FFN -> Residual -> LayerNorm For ESM2, looking at `modeling_esm.py`: ```python class EsmLayer(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.chunk_size_feed_forward = config.chunk_size_feed_forward self.seq_len_dim = 1 self.attention = EsmAttention(config) self.intermediate = EsmIntermediate(config) self.output = EsmOutput(config) self.LayerNorm = nn.LayerNorm(config.hidden_size, eps=config.layer_norm_eps) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(config.hidden_dropout_prob) def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask): attention_output = self.attention(hidden_states, attention_mask) intermediate_output = self.intermediate(attention_output) layer_output = self.output(intermediate_output, hidden_states) layer_output = self.dropout(self.LayerNorm(layer_output)) return layer_output ``` Wait, that looks like Post-LN! The LayerNorm is at the END of the layer output, after the dropout. So the sequence is: 1. Attention(hidden_states, attention_mask) -> attn_out - Inside EsmAttention: layer_norm(hidden_states) -> self_attn -> output_dense -> dropout -> + hidden_states 2. intermediate(attn_out) -> ffn1 3. output(ffn1, attn_out) -> ffn_out (includes dense2, dropout, + attn_out) 4. dropout(LayerNorm(ffn_out)) -> output If this is correct, then each EsmLayer returns a LayerNorm'd output. And the next layer starts with attention. But wait, looking at `EsmAttention`: ```python class EsmAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.self = EsmSelfAttention(config) self.output = EsmSelfOutput(config) self.pruned_heads = set() self.LayerNorm = nn.LayerNorm(config.hidden_size, eps=config.layer_norm_eps) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(config.hidden_dropout_prob) def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask): self_outputs = self.self(hidden_states, attention_mask, output_attentions=False) attention_output = self.output(self_outputs[0], hidden_states) attention_output = self.dropout(self.LayerNorm(attention_output)) outputs = (attention_output,) return outputs ``` YES! This matches. So: 1. `self.self` does `layer_norm(hidden_states) -> QKV -> attention -> output_proj` 2. `self.output` does `dropout(output_proj) + hidden_states` 3. `self.LayerNorm` is applied to the result 4. Dropout is applied So each EsmAttention returns: `dropout(LayerNorm(hidden_states + dropout(dense(attention_scores))))` For inference, dropout is identity: - `LayerNorm(hidden_states + dense(attention_scores))` Similarly, `EsmLayer` returns: - `LayerNorm(attention_output + dense2(gelu(dense1(attention_output))))` Where `attention_output` is already LayerNorm'd. So the structure is: ``` hidden = embedding(input_ids) + pos_emb for layer in layers: # Attention sublayer ln = LayerNorm(hidden) # inside EsmSelfAttention.self attn = self_attention(ln) attn_out = hidden + dense(attn) attn_out = LayerNorm(attn_out) # inside EsmAttention # FFN sublayer ffn1 = gelu(dense1(attn_out)) ffn2 = dense2(ffn1) hidden = attn_out + ffn2 hidden = LayerNorm(hidden) # inside EsmLayer # Wait, where is the final LayerNorm applied before LM head? ``` Looking at `EsmEncoder`: ```python class EsmEncoder(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.config = config self.layer = nn.ModuleList([EsmLayer(config) for _ in range(config.num_hidden_layers)]) self.emb_layer_norm_after = nn.LayerNorm(config.hidden_size, eps=config.layer_norm_eps) def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask): for i, layer_module in enumerate(self.layer): layer_outputs = layer_module(hidden_states, attention_mask) hidden_states = layer_outputs[0] if self.emb_layer_norm_after: hidden_states = self.emb_layer_norm_after(hidden_states) return (hidden_states,) ``` Wait, there's `emb_layer_norm_after`! Is this always present? Looking at ESM2 config, `is_folding_model` is typically False, but `emb_layer_norm_after` might still exist. In `EsmModel`: ```python self.encoder = EsmEncoder(config) ``` And `EsmEncoder` has `self.emb_layer_norm_after`. But is it used? Looking at the code: ```python if self.emb_layer_norm_after: hidden_states = self.emb_layer_norm_after(hidden_states) ``` Wait, I see `emb_layer_norm_after` but is this part of `EsmEncoder` or `EsmModel`? Let me check `EsmModel`: ```python class EsmModel(EsmPreTrainedModel): def __init__(self, config, add_pooling_layer=True): super().__init__(config) self.config = config self.embeddings = EsmEmbeddings(config) self.encoder = EsmEncoder(config) self.pooler = EsmPooler(config) if add_pooling_layer else None self.contact_head = EsmContactPredictionHead(config) ... class EsmEncoder(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.config = config self.layer = nn.ModuleList([EsmLayer(config) for _ in range(config.num_hidden_layers)]) self.emb_layer_norm_after = EsmLayerNorm(config.hidden_size, eps=config.layer_norm_eps) ... def forward(...): ... if self.emb_layer_norm_after: hidden_states = self.emb_layer_norm_after(hidden_states) return BaseModelOutput(...) ``` Actually, I think `emb_layer_norm_after` might be conditionally created based on config. Now, to summarize the full forward for standard ESM2 without `emb_layer_norm_before`: 1. `embeddings = word_emb[input_ids] + pos_emb[0:seq_len]` 2. `embeddings = LayerNorm(embeddings)` 3. For each layer i in 0..32: a. `self_attention_qkv = linear(embeddings, W_qkv)` b. `attn_scores = softmax( (Q @ K^T / sqrt(d_k)) + mask )` c. `attn_out = attn_scores @ V` d. `attn_proj = linear(attn_out, W_o)` e. `attn_residual = embeddings + attn_proj` f. `attn_ln = LayerNorm(attn_residual)` g. `ffn1 = linear(attn_ln, W_1)` h. `ffn1_gelu = GELU(ffn1)` i. `ffn2 = linear(ffn1_gelu, W_2)` j. `ffn_residual = attn_ln + ffn2` k. `output = LayerNorm(ffn_residual)` # This is EsmLayer.LayerNorm l. `embeddings = output` 4. `encoder_output = LayerNorm(embeddings)` # emb_layer_norm_after 5. `lm_dense = linear(encoder_output, W_lm_dense)` 6. `lm_gelu = GELU(lm_dense)` 7. `lm_ln = LayerNorm(lm_gelu)` 8. `logits = linear(lm_ln, W_lm_decoder)` Wait, I think step 4 (`emb_layer_norm_after`) is indeed applied. But hmm, is there an extra LayerNorm in `EsmAttention`? Let me re-examine: ```python class EsmAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.self = EsmSelfAttention(config) self.output = EsmSelfOutput(config) self.pruned_heads = set() self.LayerNorm = nn.LayerNorm(config.hidden_size, eps=config.layer_norm_eps) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(config.hidden_dropout_prob) def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask): self_outputs = self.self(hidden_states, attention_mask, ...) attention_output = self.output(self_outputs[0], hidden_states) attention_output = self.dropout(self.LayerNorm(attention_output)) return (attention_output,) ``` Yes, `EsmAttention` has its own `LayerNorm`. Wait, that's a lot. And each LayerNorm does a full reduction over 1280 elements. With batch*seq=65536, that's 65536 * 69 = 4.5M reductions. Each reduction reads/writes ~335MB. Total LayerNorm memory traffic: ~23GB. At 3.35TB/s, that's ~7ms. Plus overhead of kernel launches. Actually, fusing these LayerNorms with adjacent operations won't save much if they need to be separate. But some can be fused: - `residual_add + LayerNorm` saves some bandwidth. OK, now let me also think about the LM head structure. Then in `EsmEncoder.forward`: ```python extended_attention_mask: torch.Tensor = self.get_extended_attention_mask(attention_mask, input_shape) ``` And `get_extended_attention_mask`: ```python def get_extended_attention_mask(self, attention_mask, input_shape, device=None, dtype=None): if dtype is None: dtype = next(self.parameters()).dtype if attention_mask.dim() == 3: extended_attention_mask = attention_mask[:, None, :, :] elif attention_mask.dim() == 2: extended_attention_mask = attention_mask[:, None, None, :] else: raise ValueError extended_attention_mask = extended_attentionmask.to(dtype=dtype) extended_attention_mask = (1.0 - extended_attention_mask) * torch.finfo(dtype).min return extended_attention_mask ``` Wait! Let me think... no, PyTorch's `torch.finfo` maps to `torch._C._get_float_info` or similar. Looking at the Python source: `torch.finfo` returns a `FInfo` object. Other versions might call it differently. Actually, let me think about newer PyTorch. In PyTorch 2.0, there is indeed `torch.finfo(torch.float32).min`! Or maybe it's `.smallest_normal` or something else. You know what, regardless of the exact value, the attention mask is either a very large negative number or `-inf`. And in C++, we can use a large negative number like `-10000.0f` or `-1e9f` and get the same softmax behavior. Actually, wait! If the reference uses `-3.4e38`, and we use `-10000.0`, will `softmax` give the same result? For valid tokens, the attention score is typically around 0 to 0.1 (after scaling by 1/sqrt(64) = 0.125). If some scores are -10000.0, `exp(-10000)` underflows to exactly 0 in float32. The remaining valid scores might sum to a slightly different denominator if we had used `-inf`, but since `exp(-3.4e38)` ALSO underflows to 0, the results should be identical! Wait, is `exp(-3.4e38)` different from `exp(-10000.0)` in float32? Both underflow to 0. So the softmax denominator is the same. The numerator for masked positions is also 0/... = 0. So `-10000.0` should give exactly the same result as `-inf` or any sufficiently large negative number! Let me verify: in float32, the minimum positive normal number is ~1.175e-38. `exp(-88.7) ≈ 1.6e-39`. So for any x < -89, `exp(x)` underflows to 0 in float32. `-10000` gives `exp(-10000) = 0`. `-3.4e38` also gives `exp(-3.4e38) = 0` (because the argument to exp is treated as `-inf` effectively). Actually, in IEEE 754, `exp(-3.4e38)` in float32 computation might first be computed as a float32 value. `-3.4e38` is within representable range, but `exp(-3.4e38)` underflows to 0. The result is exactly 0. For `-10000.0`, `exp(-10000.0)` also underflows to 0. So as long as the masked values are less than about -89, the softmax will produce exactly the same results! Using `-10000.0` is numerically equivalent to using `-inf` for the purpose of masking in float32 softmax. This is great. We can safely use a mask value of `-1e4f` or `-1e9f` in C++. Now, for the extended attention mask shape: `[batch_size, 1, 1, seq_length]`. We can create it in C++ as: ```cpp auto mask = (input_ids != pad_token_id).to(torch::kFloat32); // [B, S] mask = mask.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(2); // [B, 1, 1, S] mask = (1.0f - mask) * -10000.0f; ``` Then during attention: ```cpp scores = scores + mask; // broadcast to [B, H, S, S] ``` This should exactly match the reference. Wait, the reference creates `extended_attention_mask` and adds it to `attention_scores`. Let me check the dtype. `scores` is float32. `mask` should also be float32. Yes. One more thing: in the reference, the attention scores are computed as: ```python query_layer = query_layer / math.sqrt(self.attention_head_size) attention_scores = torch.matmul(query_layer, key_layer.transpose(-1, -2)) ``` So the division happens BEFORE the matmul, not after. Does this matter numerically? Since `query_layer / sqrt(64)` is equivalent to `query_layer * 0.125`, and then matmul. If we do matmul first and then multiply by 0.125, the associativity differences should be minimal. But to match exactly, we should divide first then matmul. Actually, `torch.matmul(q / scale, k.T)` vs `torch.matmul(q, k.T) / scale` - the division broadcasts over the last dimension. In float32 with TF32 enabled, these might give slightly different results because of the matmul precision. To be exact, I'll divide Q by sqrt(head_dim) before the QK^T matmul. In our C++ code: ```cpp q = q * scale; // or q = q / sqrt(head_dim) scores = at::matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1)); ``` Wait, there's another subtlety. In `EsmSelfAttention`, the scaling is done before transpose_for_scores? Let me check: ```python query_layer = self.transpose_for_scores(self.query(hidden_states)) query_layer = query_layer / math.sqrt(self.attention_head_size) ``` Actually, I think the scaling is done after `transpose_for_scores`, on the reshaped tensor. So it's applied per-head. ```python query_layer = query_layer / math.sqrt(self.attention_head_size) ``` Where `query_layer` has shape [B, num_heads, S, head_dim]. So each element is divided by 8.0. If we do `q = q * scale` where `scale = 1.0f / std::sqrt(64.0f) = 0.125f`, before `matmul(q, k^T)`, this matches. OK, there's another small but important issue. In `EsmSelfAttention.transpose_for_scores`: ```python def transpose_for_scores(self, x): new_x_shape = x.size()[:-1] + (self.num_attention_heads, self.attention_head_size) x = x.view(new_x_shape) return x.permute(0, 2, 1, 3) ``` So for x of shape [B*S, hidden_size] or [B, S, hidden_size]: - new shape: [B, S, num_heads, head_dim] - permute: [B, num_heads, S, head_dim] We need to replicate this exactly. In C++: ```cpp auto q = qkv.slice(1, 0, H).view({B, S, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); ``` Wait, if `qkv` has shape [B*S, 3*H], then `qkv.slice(1, 0, H)` has shape [B*S, H]. But `view({B, S, num_heads, head_dim})` requires the total elements to match: B*S*H = B*S*num_heads*head_dim. Yes. So we need `B` and `S` as separate variables. In C++, `input_ids` has shape [B, S]. So we can get B and S from `input_ids.size(0)` and `input_ids.size(1)`. Actually, in `EsmEmbeddings`, the output has shape [B, S, H]. So hidden states have shape [B, S, H], not [B*S, H]. In the HuggingFace code, does the matmul use 2D or 3D tensors? `nn.Linear` on a 3D tensor [B, S, H] outputs [B, S, out_H]. The matmul is batched automatically. For our custom implementation, we can: - Keep tensors in 3D: [B, S, H] - For `at::addmm`, 3D tensors work if we do it batch-wise OR if we reshape to 2D first. PyTorch's `nn.Linear` on 3D input uses `addmm` internally: it flattens the first dimensions [B*S, H], does the matmul, then reshapes back. We can do the same in C++: ```cpp auto B = input_ids.size(0); auto S = input_ids.size(1); auto H = config.hidden_size; // embeddings: [B, S, H] auto hidden = at::embedding(word_embeddings, input_ids); // [B, S, H] hidden = hidden + position_embeddings.unsqueeze(0); // [1, S, H] broadcast to [B, S, H] hidden = custom_layer_norm_3d(hidden, emb_gamma, emb_beta); // For each layer for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; i++) { auto hidden_2d = hidden.view({B*S, H}); // Attention auto qkv = at::addmm(qkv_bias[i], hidden_2d, qkv_weight[i]); // [B*S, 3H] auto q = qkv.slice(1, 0, H).view({B, S, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); auto k = qkv.slice(1, H, 2*H).view({B, S, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); auto v = qkv.slice(1, 2*H, 3*H).view({B, S, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); // Scale and attention q = q * scale; auto scores = at::matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1)); // [B, H, S, S] scores = scores + attention_mask; // broadcast [B, 1, 1, S] -> [B, H, S, S] auto probs = at::softmax(scores, -1); auto attn = at::matmul(probs, v); // [B, H, S, head_dim] attn = attn.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({B*S, H}); auto attn_proj = at::addmm(attn_out_bias[i], attn, attn_out_weight[i]); // [B*S, H] // Residual + LayerNorm auto attn_ln = fused_add_layernorm(hidden_2d, attn_proj, ln_attn_gamma[i], ln_attn_beta[i], eps); // FFN auto ffn1 = at::addmm(ffn1_bias[i], attn_ln, ffn1_weight[i]); // [B*S, intermediate_size] auto ffn1_gelu = fused_bias_gelu(ffn1, ffn1_bias[i]); // Wait, addmm already added bias! ``` Wait, I used `at::addmm(ffn1_bias, attn_ln, ffn1_weight)`. `at::addmm` adds the bias. So for `fused_bias_gelu`, I should just pass `ffn1` (which already has bias). The kernel applies GELU in-place. Actually, `at::addmm(bias, input, weight)` does `input @ weight + bias`. So no need for an extra bias kernel. Just GELU. Then: ```cpp auto ffn2 = at::addmm(ffn2_bias[i], ffn1_gelu, ffn2_weight[i]); // [B*S, H] // Residual + LayerNorm hidden = fused_add_layernorm(attn_ln, ffn2, ln_out_gamma[i], ln_out_beta[i], eps); } ``` Wait, the shapes! `hidden` starts as [B, S, H]. `hidden_2d` is [B*S, H]. After `fused_add_layernorm`, the output is [B*S, H]. We can reshape back to [B, S, H] at the end of each layer or keep it as [B*S, H]. Either way works, but for consistency with the rest of the code, I'll keep it as [B, S, H] and use `view()` to reshape. Actually, `at::addmm` expects 2D inputs. So we can keep `hidden` as [B, S, H] and for matmuls do `hidden.view({B*S, H})`. For `fused_add_layernorm`, I can write a kernel that takes two [B, S, H] tensors and outputs [B, S, H]. To avoid extra reshapes, I'll just work with [B, S, H] throughout. Wait, but `qkv` needs to be reshaped to [B, S, num_heads, head_dim] anyway. So keeping [B, S, H] is natural for the sequence dimension. For the `at::addmm` calls, I can do: ```cpp auto flat_hidden = hidden.view({-1, H}); auto qkv = at::addmm(qkv_bias, flat_hidden, qkv_weight); // [B*S, 3H] ``` This is simple. Now for the fused kernels: **fused_add_layernorm kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void fused_add_layernorm_kernel(float* out, const float* a, const float* b, const float* gamma, const float* beta, int N, int C, float eps) { int idx = blockIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; const float* a_ptr = a + idx * C; const float* b_ptr = b + idx * C; float* out_ptr = out + idx * C; // Compute sum and sum of squares float sum = 0.0f, sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { float val = a_ptr[i] + b_ptr[i]; sum += val; sum_sq += val * val; out_ptr[i] = val; // Store intermediate } // Reduce within block __shared__ float s_sum, s_var; // ... warp reduction ... float mean = s_sum / C; float var = s_var / C - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { float val = out_ptr[i]; out_ptr[i] = (val - mean) * inv_std * gamma[i] + beta[i]; } } ``` Wait, but writing this kernel with proper warp reductions is a bit involved. And I need to make sure it's numerically equivalent to PyTorch. For simplicity and correctness, we could just use ATen's operations in C++ without custom fusions. We would still gain from: 1. No Python overhead between operations 2. Fewer temporary allocations if we reuse buffers But honestly, without actual custom kernel fusions, the speedup might not be huge. It's basically a C++ reimplementation of eager mode. Hmm, but there's another benefit: in C++, we can control exactly which operations are used and avoid some of PyTorch's overhead like dynamic dispatch, shape checks, etc. Actually, PyTorch's C++ frontend (`libtorch`) is quite fast. It still does shape checks but avoids Python overhead. Combined with QKV fusion, the speedup could be meaningful: - 3 GEMMs -> 1 GEMM saves 2 kernel launches per layer = 66 fewer launches - Plus C++ overhead reduction = maybe 5-10ms - Plus we eliminate intermediate tensors created by Python's `nn.Module` wrappers This might give us 10-20% speedup, which could be enough to pass. Wait, but actually the HuggingFace `EsmSelfAttention` might have been updated to use `scaled_dot_product_attention`, which changes things. Let me think... PyTorch doesn't provide a fused `add + layer_norm` operation. We could write a custom kernel that does: ```cuda sum = a + b ln = layer_norm(sum, gamma, beta) ``` This saves reading/writing `sum` compared to doing `add` then `layer_norm`. But we can implement this as a custom CUDA kernel! This is a great custom kernel that: 1. Is actually useful 2. Fuses operations that PyTorch doesn't fuse 3. Saves memory bandwidth Let me design this kernel: For input [B, S, H] shaped tensors `a` and `b`, and per-channel `gamma`, `beta`: ``` output[b, s, h] = ((a[b, s, h] + b[b, s, h]) - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma[h] + beta[h] where mean = sum_h(a + b) / H and var = sum_h((a + b - mean)^2) / H ``` This is exactly PyTorch LayerNorm but fused with the addition. Implementation: - Launch B*S blocks - Each block has H threads (or shared H with loop due to H=1280) - Each thread computes partial sums - Block reduction for sum and sum_sq - Then normalize Wait, 1280 elements is a lot for a single block if we want to use shared memory. But H100 has 1024 threads per block max. So we'd need to handle 1280 with a loop: - 256 threads per block - Each thread handles 5 elements (256 * 5 = 1280) - Compute partial sum and sum_sq - Store intermediate values in shared memory or registers - Reduce across 256 threads - Then apply normalization in second loop This is a standard kernel pattern. Similarly, we could write a `fused_add` kernel, but `at::add` is already very optimized. For GELU, we can write a custom kernel that applies the tanh approximation formula: ```cuda __device__ inline float gelu_tanh(float x) { float x3 = x * x * x; float t = tanhf(0.79788456f * (x + 0.044715f * x3)); return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + t); } ``` This is more precise than using `at::gelu` because we control the exact formula. But actually, we might not even need custom CUDA kernels. We could write our C++ forward pass and put ALL the code in `cpp_sources`, with no `cuda_sources`. But `cpp_sources` with `.cpp` extension is compiled by the C++ compiler, while `cuda_sources` with `.cu` extension is compiled by nvcc. Now, let me think about the overall code organization. **Python file structure:** ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from transformers import AutoModelForMaskedLM, AutoConfig # Load the CUDA extension cuda_sources = r''' // Custom CUDA kernels __global__ void fused_add_layernorm_kernel(...) { ... } __global__ void gelu_tanh_kernel(...) { ... } ''' cpp_sources = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <ATen/ATen.h> // C++ wrappers that launch CUDA kernels torch::Tensor fused_add_layernorm(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b, torch::Tensor gamma, torch::Tensor beta, float eps) { ... } torch::Tensor gelu_tanh(torch::Tensor x) { ... } // Global state for model weights struct ESM2Weights { ... }; ESM2Weights g_weights; void init_model(...) { g_weights = ...; } torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor input_ids) { // Use g_weights and ATen ops to compute forward } ''' # Actually, load_inline needs to be called at module level esm_cuda = torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline( name="esm2_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_sources, cuda_sources=cuda_sources, functions=["init_model", "forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() model = AutoModelForMaskedLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) model.eval() # Extract weights by inspecting model structure # Call esm_cuda.init_model(...) def forward(self, x): return esm_cuda.forward(x) ``` Wait, I need to be very careful about passing the weights. The `init_model` function needs to accept all the weights. With 33 layers and ~8 tensors per layer plus embeddings and LM head, that's 33*8 + 6 = 270 tensors. Passing them individually to a C++ function is impossible unless we use a list/dict. With `load_inline`, can we pass a Python list of tensors to C++ `std::vector<torch::Tensor>`? I believe yes, pybind11 handles this. But `pybind11` needs to be able to convert `list` to `std::vector<torch::Tensor>`. With the auto-generated bindings from `load_inline`, I think it works for basic types but I'm not 100% sure about `std::vector<torch::Tensor>` with auto-generated bindings. Actually, looking at the `load_inline` implementation, it uses `m.def(name, &func)`. For pybind11, this automatically handles standard conversions. `std::vector<torch::Tensor>` should bind to `list[torch.Tensor]` in Python. To be safe, we can flatten all weights into a few big tensors and pass them as arguments. Or we can pass just the model parameters as a single dictionary, but pybind11 with auto-generated bindings might not handle `std::map`. Actually, a safer approach: pass each category as a separate `torch::Tensor` that contains stacked weights. For example: - `qkv_weights`: [33, 1280, 3840] tensor (all layers stacked) - `qkv_biases`: [33, 3840] - `attn_out_weights`: [33, 1280, 1280] - `attn_out_biases`: [33, 1280] - `ln_attn_gammas`: [33, 1280] - `ln_attn_betas`: [33, 1280] - `ffn1_weights`: [33, 1280, 5120] - `ffn1_biases`: [33, 5120] - `ffn2_weights`: [33, 5120, 1280] - `ffn2_biases`: [33, 1280] - `ln_out_gammas`: [33, 1280] - `ln_out_betas`: [33, 1280] Then in C++: ```cpp auto qkv_w = qkv_weights[i]; // [1280, 3840] ``` This is MUCH cleaner. We pass ~15 tensors from Python to C++, and `init_model` accepts these 15 tensors. Then `forward(torch::Tensor input_ids)` uses the globally stored stacked weights. This approach is much better than passing lists. Let me also include the embedding weights and LM head weights: - `word_embeddings`: [vocab_size, H] - `position_embeddings`: [max_position, H] - `emb_ln_gamma`: [H] - `emb_ln_beta`: [H] - `emb_layer_norm_after_gamma`: [H] - `emb_layer_norm_after_beta`: [H] - `lm_dense_weight`: [H, H] - `lm_dense_bias`: [H] - `lm_ln_gamma`: [H] - `lm_ln_beta`: [H] - `lm_decoder_weight`: [H, vocab_size] - `lm_decoder_bias`: [vocab_size] Total: ~27 tensors passed to init_model. Very manageable. One issue: the vocab indices in `input_ids` might include `embedding.padding_idx`. `nn.Embedding` handles this by setting the row at `padding_idx` to zeros (or almost zeros, actually it might be trained). `at::embedding` does the same. We need to pass the `padding_idx` which is 1 for ESM2. So in Python init, we extract `model.config.pad_token_id = 1` and pass it to a `set_config` function, or we hardcode it.
